# 16:1
## iq2r
This idiom is used to introduce a special message from God. See how you translated it in [Jeremiah 1:4](../01/04.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh gave me a message. He said,” or “Yahweh spoke this message to me:” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 16:4
## bdx2
“They will die from deadly diseases”

## nm5s
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “No one will mourn for them or bury them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## paq4
The sons and daughters born in the land are compared to dung on the ground to emphasize that they will be disgusting and that no one will bury them. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Jeremiah 8:2](../08/02.md). (See: [[figs-simile]])

## b995
The phrase “come to an end” is a mild way to say that they will die. The word “sword” represents an army whose soldiers fight with swords. The phrase represents dying in battle. Alternate translation: “they will die in battle or from famine” (See: [[figs-euphemism]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 16:5
## irw5
This idiom is used to introduce a special message from God. See how you translated it in [Jeremiah 1:4](../01/04.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh gave me a message. He said, ‘Do” or “Yahweh spoke this message to me: ‘Do” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## byl9
The word “mourning” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “where people are mourning” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## e7zp
Yahweh speaks of no longer acting towards the people with peace, steadfast love, and mercy as if these were items that he has taken away from them. Alternate translation: “I will no longer act towards them with peace … or with steadfast love and mercy” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## fg62
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 16:6
## ngj4
This refers to all kinds of people and uses size to refer to how important they are. Alternate translation: “Both the important and the unimportant” or “Many people, regardless of how important they are, will” (See: [[figs-merism]])

## k2n5
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “No one will bury them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## s8kp
Cutting oneself and shaving one’s head were symbolic actions that expressed strong grief, especially when a loved one died. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 16:7
## t3iq
These two clauses have a similar meaning. God said basically the same thing twice for emphasis. It was customary to take food or wine to people whose relative had died. Yahweh has removed all comfort from the people because of their sin. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## ae18
Here the word “cup” represents the drink that the cup holds. Alternate translation: “none must give a comforting drink” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 16:8
## rzm9
“a house where people are feasting”

# 16:9
## z6t7
Jeremiah often uses these words to introduce an important message from Yahweh. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 6:6](../06/06.md).

## hq3r
“Look” or “Listen” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

## fxi9
Here the word “your” is plural and refers to the people of Israel. The idiom means that Yahweh will do this where they can see. Alternate translation: “in front of you” or “where you can see” (See: [[figs-you]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## b3s5
This idiom means during their lifetime. Alternate translation: “during your lifetime” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## lki1
See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 7:34](../07/34.md).

# 16:11
## fq83
The words “bowed down to” mean basically the same thing as “worshiped” and describe the posture that people used in worship. God said essentially the same thing twice for emphasis. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 16:12
## cu97
“ancestors. Look at these people and you will see that each person”

## c169
Yahweh speaks of a person’s actions as if the person were walking along a path. Here the word “heart” represents the mind or will. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Jeremiah 11:8](../11/08.md). Alternate translation: “stubbornly doing the wicked things that he wants to do” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## qh3g
“who does what I tell him to do”

# 16:13
## y41b
Yahweh speaks of forcefully removing the people from the land as if he were throwing them from the land. Alternate translation: “force you to leave this land and go” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## e16l
The mention of both “day” and “night” means all the time. Alternate translation: “all the time” or “continually” (See: [[figs-merism]])

# 16:14
## em2d
The word “behold” here alerts us to pay attention to the surprising information that follows.

## l615
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “when people will no longer say” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## xr81
“As surely as Yahweh is alive.” The people use this expression to show that what they say next is certainly true. This is a way of making a solemn promise. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 4:2](../04/02.md). Alternate translation: “I solemnly swear” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 16:16
## mt7v
Yahweh speaks of enemy armies who will attack and kill the people as if they were fishermen who are catching fish. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## mx2n
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## y22w
Yahweh speaks of enemy armies who will attack and kill the people as if they were hunters who are hunting animals. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 16:17
## l1xj
Here the word “eye” represents Yahweh who sees all that they do. Alternate translation: “I am watching everything they do” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## c7ju
Here the word “they” may refer either to the people or to their actions. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “they cannot hide from me” or “they cannot hide their ways from me” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## f9r8
Here the word “eyes” represents Yahweh who sees. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “They cannot conceal their iniquity from me” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 16:18
## tpe4
Yahweh speaks of causing the land to be unacceptable to him as if the people had polluted the land. The words “their polluting” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “because they have polluted my land” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## sq29
The idiom “to fill” means that they have placed many idols in the land. The words “their filling” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “because they have filled my inheritance with their disgusting idols” or “because their disgusting idols are everywhere in my inheritance” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## wkp2
Yahweh speaks of the land as if it were his inheritance that he has claimed as a permanent possession. Alternate translation: “the land that is my inheritance” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 16:19
## rt34
Here Jeremiah begins speaking to Yahweh.

## y75t
Jeremiah speaks of Yahweh as a place where enemies cannot attack him. He repeats the same idea three times. (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-doublet]])

## x7cn
Here the word “nations” represents the people from the nations. Alternate translation: “The people from the nations will go to you” or “The people of the nations will come to you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-go]])

## nk1x
This is an idiom that means the farthest places on the earth. By referencing both ends, it refers to everywhere in between. Alternate translation: “the farthest places on the earth” or “everywhere on earth” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-merism]])

## c5xl
Here the word “deceit” refers to false gods. Alternate translation: “our ancestors inherited nothing but false gods” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## jrc3
Here the words “They” and “them” refer to the false gods that the ancestors taught them to believe in. The two phrases mean basically the same thing, with the second explaining how they “are empty.” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 16:20
## y3fg
The people ask this rhetorical question to emphasize that people cannot make gods for themselves. It can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “People cannot make gods for themselves. The things that they make are not gods” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 16:21
## gwn6
“Therefore, indeed.” Here Yahweh begins speaking. The word “see” adds emphasis to what follows.

## fz8r
Here the word “them” refers to the people from the nations. Yahweh repeats this phrase for emphasis.

## cs55
Here the word “hand” refers to power and authority. The two phrases mean basically the same thing and emphasize Yahweh’s great power. Alternate translation: “my great power” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-doublet]])

## hl7n
Here the word “name” refers to Yahweh’s entire person. Alternate translation: “they will know that I am Yahweh, the true God” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 17:intro
## l48r
See [[JER-l48r]]
# 17:1
## f2as
See [[JER-f2as]]
## qcz2
Yahweh speaks of Judah’s sins being so great that they cannot stop committing them as if someone had engraved their sin permanently. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ntb1
Here the word “Judah” represents the people of Judah. This may be expressed in active form. Alternate translation: “Someone has written the sin of Judah” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## r9u2
This may be expressed in active form. Alternate translation: “Someone has engraved it” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## t19m
The sinful habits of the people are spoken of as if their sins had been engraved on their own hearts. The word “hearts” refers to the whole person: their thoughts, emotions, and actions. Alternate translation: “engraved in their very beings” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## gc1k
The word “horns” refers to the projections on the corners of the altars.

# 17:3
## ve7m
The words “wealth” and “treasures” mean the same thing and refer to anything that they consider valuable. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## gfz3
This refers to things that people steal or take by force.

# 17:4
## mei2
Yahweh speaks of the land as if it were an inheritance that he had given to the people of Judah as a permanent possession. Alternate translation: “You will lose the land that I have given to you as an inheritance” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## w768
Yahweh speaks of the fierceness of his anger as if his anger were a fire that burns those with whom he is angry. Alternate translation: “you have made me so angry that my anger will be like a fire that will burn forever” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 17:5
## zp54
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will curse any person who trusts in mankind” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## sd5y
Here the word “flesh” represents humans. Alternate translation: “he depends on mere humans for strength” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## f1ec
Here the word “heart” refers to the thoughts and emotions. Alternate translation: “turns his devotion away from Yahweh” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 17:6
## wqm5
The person who trusts in man instead of Yahweh will be like a plant that struggles to survive in an infertile land. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## d8k3
The phrase “barren land without inhabitants” describes and means basically the same thing as “wilderness.” Alternate translation: “the barren wilderness where no person lives” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 17:8
## rs4s
The person who trusts in Yahweh will always prosper, just like a tree does when it is planted by a river. It is not affected when there is no rain. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “he will be like a tree that someone has planted by water” (See: [[figs-simile]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## pf89
Yahweh speaks of a tree that draws its water from a stream being unaffected by heat and drought as if the tree were unafraid of these things. Alternate translation: “The heat will not affect it when it comes … No harm is caused to it in a year of drought” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 17:9
## q56q
Here the word “heart” refers to the minds and thoughts of people. Alternate translation: “The human mind is more deceitful” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## h5pr
The speaker uses this rhetorical question to emphasize that no one can understand the human heart. It can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “no one can understand it.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 17:10
## per1
Here the word “hearts” represents the emotions. Alternate translation: “who tests the emotions” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## zu1h
Here a person’s behavior is spoken of as if it were the paths along which he walks. Alternate translation: “according to how he lives” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## hlu7
Here the results of a person’s actions are spoken of as if they were fruit. Alternate translation: “what he has done” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 17:11
## r1bc
This analogy of a bird that hatches another bird’s eggs is meant to illustrate a rich man who makes his money by robbing others.

## j8my
Here the word “days” stands for someone’s entire life. Alternate translation: “when he has lived only half of his life” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## t4k5
The riches are spoken of as if they were servants who would abandon their owner. Alternate translation: “he will lose his wealth” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## wwb2
“at the end of his life”

# 17:12
## c58h
Jeremiah speaks of the temple being a “glorious throne” because it is there that Yahweh dwells and rules. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## mmb5
This refers to Mount Zion in Jerusalem.

# 17:13
## j27x
Here the word “you” refers to Yahweh.

## tkv3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “you will write in the dust those in the land who turn away from you” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## e5ej
Possible meanings are (1) this is a metaphor in which Yahweh speaks of people dying and being forgotten as if someone writes their names in the dust, and those names quickly disappear. Alternate translation: “will disappear like names that someone writes in the dust” or (2) the word “earth” is a metonym for the place of the dead. The phrase is a metaphor in which Yahweh speaks of people going to the place of the dead as if someone enrolled their names in the records of those who have died. Alternate translation: “will join those in the place of the dead” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## vf2c
Jeremiah speaks of Yahweh being the source of life as if he were a fountain of living waters. Alternate translation: “Yahweh, who is like a fountain of living waters” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## pj3q
Jeremiah speaks of fresh, running water as if it were living water. Alternate translation: “the fountain of fresh, running water” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 17:14
## emc7
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “you really will have healed me … you really will have rescued me” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## t5hz
Possible meanings are (1) “For you are the one whom I praise” or (2) “For you are the reason I sing praise.”

# 17:15
## c8ap
“Look” or “Listen” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you.”

## kn8p
Here the word “me” refers to Jeremiah and the word “they” to his enemies.

## qef1
The people use this question to ridicule Jeremiah because the things that he said had not yet happened. It can also be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Where are the things that Yahweh told you would happen?” or “The things that Yahweh told you would happen have not happened.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## s66x
“Let what Yahweh has said happen”

# 17:16
## dye9
Jeremiah speaks of leading and caring for the people as if he were a shepherd caring for sheep. He speaks of his abandoning that work as if he were running away from it. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## vlg8
“I did not desire”

## m8x1
Here the word “lips” represents Jeremiah who spoke. The word “proclamations” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “the proclamations that I spoke” or “the things that I proclaimed” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## hkb7
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I made them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 17:17
## q4lu
Jeremiah speaks of Yahweh as a place where enemies cannot attack him. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ls2i
“in times of calamity”

# 17:18
## p168
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Bring shame on my pursuers, but do not bring shame on me” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## us8n
This phrase means basically the same thing as the previous one and adds emphasis to Jeremiah’s prayer. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Make them very afraid, but do not make me afraid” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## xrc7
Jeremiah speaks of Yahweh destroying his enemies as if they were objects that Yahweh would shatter. The idiom “a double share” means to give twice as much. Alternate translation: “shatter them with complete destruction” or “destroy them twice as much” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 17:19
## ab1v
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. Alternate translation: “then go and stand in all the other gates of Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 17:21
## jye6
“to protect your lives”

# 17:23
## cu16
These two phrases mean basically the same thing and emphasize that the people refused to listen. Alternate translation: “They refused to listen” or “They refused to obey” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## ksk9
Jeremiah speaks of the people being stubborn as if they had made their necks stiff and unmovable. Alternate translation: “became stubborn” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 17:25
## i3jj
Here the throne that the king of Judah sits on is represented as “David’s throne.” See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 13:13](../13/13.md). Alternate translation: “those who sit on Judah’s throne” or “the kings of the nation of Judah” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## r4s3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “people will inhabit this city forever” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 17:27
## xjp9
“I will set the gates of Jerusalem on fire”

## r5tg
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “people cannot put it out” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 18:intro
## z3ii
See [[JER-z3ii]]
# 18:1
## ad7f
This idiom is used to introduce a special message from God. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Jeremiah 7:1](../07/01.md). Alternate translation: “This is the message that Yahweh gave to Jeremiah. He said, ‘Arise” or “This is the message that Yahweh spoke to Jeremiah: ‘Arise” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 18:2
## ww5x
“the workshop of the potter.” A potter is someone who makes pots from clay.

# 18:3
## z9ps
The word “behold” alerts us to a new person in the story. Your language may have a way of doing this.

## x9yr
The potter’s wheel is a small table that spins. A potter uses it to make pots.

# 18:4
## j5pm
The word “spoiled” means that something about the pot made the potter unhappy. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “the pot that the potter was making with his hands was not good” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## ffd2
“was forming” or “was shaping”

## qs6u
He stopped making the pot, crushed the clay back into a lump, and then used that lump of clay to make another pot.

## kd4f
Here the word “eyes” represents sight and sight represents one’s opinion. Alternate translation: “that he thought was good” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 18:5
## gj6x
This idiom is used to introduce a special message from God. See how you translated it in [Jeremiah 1:4](../01/04.md). Alternate translation: “Yahweh gave me a message. He said,” or “Yahweh spoke this message to me:” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 18:6
## y4ia
With this question, Yahweh emphasizes his authority to do as he pleases with Israel. Alternate translation: “I am allowed to act toward you, house of Israel, like the potter acts toward the clay.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## j2v5
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house. In this case it refers to the kingdom of Israel. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 3:18](../03/18.md). Alternate translation: “Israel” or “kingdom of Israel” or “people of Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## elt6
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## zg76
“Pay attention, because what I am about to say is both true and important”

## uzq5
Yahweh compares his being able to remake Israel as he see fit to the way that the potter is able to remake a lump of clay as he sees fit. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 18:7
## jk3p
These two expressions basically mean the same thing. In the first expression, Yahweh speaks of destroying a kingdom as if the kingdom were a plant or a building that he tears down. (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:8
## lnu4
“withhold” or “not bring”

# 18:9
## n3us
These two phrases mean basically the same thing. Yahweh speaks of strengthening kingdoms as if they were buildings that he would build, and as if they were plants that he would plant. (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:10
## q9ah
Here the word “it” refers to a nation or kingdom, which is a metonym for the people in that nation or kingdom. The word “eyes” represents seeing, and seeing represents thoughts or judgment. Alternate translation: “if the people of that nation do things that I consider to be evil” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## u2ti
Here the word “voice” represents the words that Yahweh speaks. Here, “not listening” is an idiom that means that they do not obey. Alternate translation: “not obeying what I say” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 18:11
## h8xz
This tells the reader to pay special attention to what follows. Alternate translation: “Look” or “Listen” or “Pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

## tj92
Yahweh speaks of planning disaster as if disaster were an object that he forms, like the potter would form clay. The second sentence repeats the same idea as the first using different words. Alternate translation: “I am about to devise a plan to bring disaster against you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-parallelism]])

## v6ut
“make a plan”

## me21
Yahweh speaks of a person’s lifestyle as if it were a “way” or path along which the person walks. Alternate translation: “his wicked way of living” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## w5eq
The words “ways” and “practices” both refer to a person’s actions and general lifestyle. Alternate translation: “so your actions will result in good happening to you” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 18:12
## fq15
The word “they” refers to the inhabitants of Judah and Jerusalem.

## nmb4
Possible meanings for this idiom are (1) the people do not care about what Jeremiah will say to them. Alternate translation: “We do not care what you say” or (2) The people do not think that anything good will come of what Jeremiah tells them to do. Alternate translation: “What you are saying will not work” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## yc7y
Here the word “heart” represents the person’s mind or will. Alternate translation: “what his evil, stubborn mind desires” or “whatever evil things that we stubbornly want to do” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 18:13
## w781
Here the word “nations” represents the people in the nations. Yahweh asks this question to scold the people of Judah. Alternate translation: “Ask the nations if any of them have ever heard of such a thing as this.” or “Nowhere on earth has anyone ever heard of such a thing like this.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## vta7
It was common to refer to nations as if they were women. Yahweh speaks of Israel being pure and faithful towards him as if she were a virgin and of Israel being unfaithful to him as if she had “committed a horrible act.” Alternate translation: “Israel, who is like a virgin, has done something horrible” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:14
## bbi9
Yahweh asks this rhetorical question to emphasize that the snow on the mountains never melts. Alternate translation: “The snow in Lebanon never leaves the rocky hills on its sides.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## pn6p
“the sides of the rocky hills”

## utv2
Yahweh asks this rhetorical question to emphasize that the mountain streams never cease to flow. He speaks of the streams drying up as if they were destroyed. Alternate translation: “Those cold mountain streams that come from far away never cease to flow.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:15
## k5st
Yahweh speaks of a person’s lifestyle as if it were a path along which he walks, and of living unfaithfully to him as if the person stumbled along the path. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “this caused them to stumble in their paths” or “it is like they stumbled while walking on a path” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## x24p
Yahweh speaks of a person’s lifestyle as if it were a path along which he walks. Here, “the ancient paths” represents the way that Yahweh had told their ancestors to live and “lesser paths” represents living unfaithfully to Yahweh. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:16
## qh44
The word “horror” may be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “will become something that horrifies people” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## g6ye
The word “hissing” refers to a sound that shows strong disapproval and can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “an object at which people will always hiss”

## mx2e
Here the word “her” refers to “Their land.”

# 18:17
## n6hk
Yahweh speaks of causing the people to flee before their enemies as if he were an eastern wind that scatters dust and debris. Alternate translation: “I will be like an eastern wind and scatter them before their enemies” or “I will scatter them before their enemies like an eastern wind scatters dust and debris” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## n2xm
Here the word “them” refers to “my people.”

## mn5a
“I will turn my back toward them, and not my face.” Turning one’s back towards someone is a symbolic action that represents rejection and turning one’s face towards someone is a symbolic action that represents favor. Alternate translation: “I will reject them and not act favorably towards them” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 18:18
## vh9w
“let us make plans to harm Jeremiah”

## cf4s
The words “will never perish” can be supplied to each of these phrases. This can also be expressed in positive terms. Alternate translation: “the law will never perish from the priests, and advice will never perish from the wise men, and words will never perish from the prophets” or “the priests will always have the law, the wise men will always give advice, and the prophets will always speak” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## f353
Here “words” represents messages that the prophets speak from Yahweh. Alternate translation: “words of Yahweh from the prophets” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## ay55
The people speak of speaking harmful words against Jeremiah as if their words were weapons with which they attack him. Alternate translation: “say things that will harm him” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:19
## u46b
Here Jeremiah begins speaking to Yahweh.

# 18:20
## p988
Jeremiah asks this question to emphasize that good actions should not be repaid with bad things. Alternate translation: “Disaster from them should not be my reward for being good to them.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## t4h5
Jeremiah speaks of his enemies planning to kill him as if they had dug a pit in which to trap him. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## p3xy
Here the word “fury” represents the punishment that Yahweh intended to inflict upon them. Jeremiah speaks of Yahweh not punishing them as if he were turning his fury away from them. Alternate translation: “so that you would not punish them in your anger” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 18:21
## xrk4
The idiom “give over” means to place a person in the power of another person. Jeremiah speaks of “famine” as if it were a person who could have power over others. Alternate translation: “cause their children to die from famine” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-personification]])

## x3mp
Here the word “hands” represents power. Alternate translation: “cause those who use the sword to have power over them” or “cause them to die in battle” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## c2vn
“let the children and husbands of their women die.” The word “bereaved” refers to a woman whose children have all died.

## yp4k
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “let people kill their men” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## gz9s
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “let people kill their young men with swords in battle” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 18:22
## nj6w
The phrase “a distressed shout” can be translated with a verbal phrase. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Let people hear a distressed shout” or “Let people hear others shout in distress” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## zdc2
These two phrases share similar meanings. Jeremiah speaks of his enemies planning to kill him as if they had dug a pit for him to fall into and set traps in which to capture him. (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## l4rh
See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 18:20](../18/20.md).

## pxk9
Here the word “feet” represents Jeremiah. Alternate translation: “have hidden traps for me” or “have hidden traps for me to step on” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 18:23
## yh1m
Jeremiah speaks of Yahweh forgiving his enemies’ sins as if someone had written their sins down and Yahweh would erase them. This phrase means the same thing as the previous phrase. Alternate translation: “Do not pardon their sins” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## fin3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “let people overthrow them in your presence” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## jrr6
The word “wrath” can be translated with a verb. Alternate translation: “when you are still angry with them” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

Stocks are a wooden frame with holes in it that people use to lock up a prisoner’s hands, feet, and head.

This gate is different from the gate in the city wall that shared the same name.

## j1gs
Placing things in people’s hands represents giving thing to the people or allowing the people to take the things. Alternate translation: “I will give these things to your enemies” or “I will allow your enemies to take possession of these things” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 20:8
## dqv6

## bd79
Here “word” refers to the message of Yahweh. The words “reproach” and “mocking” can be translated with verbal phrases. Alternate translation: “people reproach and mock me every day because I proclaim Yahweh’s message” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## t4n7
The words “reproach” and “mocking” mean basically the same thing and emphasize that people have ridiculed Jeremiah for proclaiming Yahweh’s message. Alternate translation: “a reason for people to ridicule me” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## bs6s

## a3zh
Here the words “heart” and “bones” represent Jeremiah’s innermost being. Jeremiah speaks of being unable to not speak Yahweh’s message as if Yahweh’s message were a fire that burns within him. Alternate translation: “Yahweh’s word is like a fire that burns deep within me” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## zc9e
Jeremiah uses this rhetorical question to complain that there was no good reason for him to be born. It can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “There was no reason for me to be born only to see troubles and agony … shame.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## tf6j

## hc2w

All of these phrases refer to Nebuchadnezzar and his army. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## z1zk
“wanting to kill them”

## equ4
Possible meanings are (1) this is a metonym for death in battle. Alternate translation: “in battle” or (2) they will be killed by literal swords. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## j95p
These three phrases have the same meaning and emphasize that Nebuchadnezzar will deal severely with them. Alternate translation: “He will not show them any mercy or compassion at all” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 21:8
## uz34
“the people of Jerusalem”

## k2vf
Yahweh is offering the people of Jerusalem a choice that will determine whether they live or die.

# 21:9
## uw2k
Death by the sword refers to death in battle. Alternate translation: “in battle and by famine and plague” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## fn95
This symbolic act represents surrender. Alternate translation: “surrendering to” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## g472
“attacked you from all sides”

## as7l
The one who surrenders to the Babylonians will escape with his life, even though he will lose all his possessions.

# 21:10
## s4dv
This idiom means he “firmly decided.” Alternate translation: “I have made up my mind to oppose this city” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## e61c
“am staring angrily at”

## wwu1
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## jk2w
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I have given it” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## sef1
Here “hand” represents the power of the king to destroy the city. Alternate translation: “into the power of the king” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 21:11
## u3ev
This may serve as a title for 21:12 - 23:8. Alternate translation: “Listen to what Yahweh says about the king of Judah, his family, and his servants”

## l7p3
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house. In this case it refers to king and his family. Alternate translation: “the king of Judah and his family” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 21:12
## nl9b
This refers to the same people as “the house of the king of Judah.” All of the kings of Judah were descendants of David. Alternate translation: “Descendants of David” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## s22h
The abstract noun “justice” can be stated as an action. Alternate translation: “Always treat the people whom you rule over fairly” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## l5bg
Here “hand” refers to power. Alternate translation: “the power of the one who oppresses them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## bpj8
Here Yahweh’s punishment is spoken of as if it was a fire that would burn up those who did evil. Alternate translation: “I will punish and destroy you in my anger quickly and completely” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ph8p
Jeremiah continues the simile of Yahweh’s punishment as a fire that was so hot that no one could put it out with water. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 21:13
## a8zt
This alerts whoever is hearing to pay special attention to what follows. Alternate translation: “Pay attention”

## qie5
Possible meanings are (1) this may refer to Jerusalem, which was located above a valley, because “inhabitant” is singular and feminine, as is Jerusalem or (2) this is directed to another city or group of people. Most versions leave the original form rather than specifying the city.

## h96i
“I oppose” or “I am going to punish”

## dxt4
These questions are used to state their confidence that they can resist attack. Alternate translation: “No one will attack us! … No one will enter our houses!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 21:14
## uf3g
The result of their evil deeds is spoken of as fruit which grew from those practices. Alternate translation: “I will punish you as you deserve because of the things you have done” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## nf8v
Possible meanings are (1) this is a metaphor for the columns of wood that supported the palace or (2) these are literal patches of brush. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:intro
## in1u
See [[JER-in1u]]
# 22:1
## z5bz
“the palace of the king of Judah.” The word “house” has a range of possible meanings. In this case it refers to the royal palace where the king lived.

## qfg5
“proclaim this message”

# 22:2
## qj9k
These gates are the gates of the king’s palace. Alternate translation: “your people who come to visit the king” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 22:3
## qb4x
The abstract nouns “justice” and “righteousness can be stated as actions. Alternate translation: “Act fairly and justly” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## m8e5
“rescue anyone who has been robbed from the hand of the oppressor”

## y9er
Here “the hand” refers to power or control. Alternate translation: “the power of the oppressor” or “the one who would harm him” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## m38q
“Do not treat badly”

## z2z9
a child who does not have parents

## b6c5
Here “pour out … blood” is associated with murder, and “blood” refers to the murdered people. Alternate translation: “Do not … murder innocent people” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-synecdoche]])

## t9h8
Possible meanings are (1) Jerusalem or (2) the whole country of Judah. This does not imply that it is good to murder people in other places.

# 22:4
## f37x
The metonym “throne” refers to kingly authority like David had. Alternate translation: “kings, ruling like David before them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## vfy3
This refers to the gates to the courtyard of the palace.

## l5m1
This phrase is a metonym that describes the kings as powerful and wealthy. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## rx2m
This sentence lists all who will be powerful and wealthy. This can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “he, his servants, and his people will ride into the palace on chariots and horses” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 22:5
## zef9
“if you refuse to pay attention to” or “if you do not obey”

## w37m
This phrase here appears to refer specifically to the royal palace.

# 22:6
## hfy3
Possible meanings are (1) this refers to the royal palace where the king lived or (2) “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, who were the royal line of the kings of Judah. Alternate translation: “the royal dynasty of Judah” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## v879
Possible meanings are (1) “You are as beautiful as Gilead or the summit of Lebanon” or (2) “You give me as much pleasure as Gilead or the summit of Lebanon.” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## ahd6
What was formerly beautiful is said to become bare and deserted. Alternate translation: “cause you to become empty like the desert” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:7
## wcw6
“I have chosen an army to attack you”

## d1mn
Possible meaning are (1) the beams of the palace or (2) the leading men of the royal family. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## wb7h
Possible meanings are (1) a literal fire that will burn the palace beams or (2) the destruction of the royal family is spoken of as if they were burned in a fire. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:8
## t15z
Here “nations” refers to the people from those nations that pass by. Alternate translation: “Then many people from various nations will pass by this city” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 22:9
## a4v3
These two phrases mean the same thing. The phrase “bowed down” describes the posture that people used in worship. Alternate translation: “they worshiped other gods” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[translate-symaction]])

# 22:10
## mwb9
See [[JER-mwb9]]
## n3gc
This is singular, and refers to King Josiah who had been killed.

## nz1s
This refers to King Jehoahaz who was taken into exile in Egypt.

## mc5c
“never come back and see the land of Israel again” or “never see his homeland again”

# 22:11
## gm4b
The name in Hebrew is “Shallum,” but he is better known as Jehoahaz. (See: [[translate-names]])

## rw5q
“who became king when his father, Josiah, died”

# 22:13
## qz4r
The words “him” and “his” and “who” and “he” refer to King Jehoahaz (verse 11).

## qb7j
The abstract nouns “unrighteousness” and “injustice” have similar meanings, are repeated for emphasis, and can be stated as actions. Alternate translation: “unjustly forced men to build his house and his upper rooms” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## rzx8
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house. In this case it refers to Jehoahaz (verse 11) and his family. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## b5uz
This refers to any of the neighbors of King Jehoahaz (verse 11) whom the king forces to work.

# 22:14
## m567
These refer to King Jehoahaz (verse 11).

## ub8w
These characteristics all describe a very expensive house.

# 22:15
## ag7u
Yahweh asks this rhetorical question to rebuke Jehoiakim for his expensive palace. Alternate translation: “Having a cedar palace does not make you a good king.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## b88r
Yahweh asks this rhetorical question to remind Jehoiakim about the example of his father, King Josiah. Alternate translation: “Your father King Josiah enjoyed his life, yet did … righteousness.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## y86v
These abstract nouns have similar meanings, are repeated for emphasis and can be stated as actions. Alternate translation: “act justly and fairly” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 22:16
## nkx5
“Things went well for him”

## y4lk
Yahweh asks this rhetorical question to rebuke Jehoiakim for not following the example of his father, King Josiah. Alternate translation: “This is what it means to know me.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## rd6y
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 22:17
## tab3
Here “eyes” is a metonym for seeing and “heart” is a metonym for thinking. Alternate translation: “you never look for or think about anything except … crushing of others” or “you only look for and think about ways to cheat others and pour out innocent blood, and to oppress and crush others” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## u23t
This is getting money by cheating or by using unfair means.

## idh3
Here “pouring out … blood” is associated with murder, and “blood” refers to the murdered people. Alternate translation: “murdering innocent people” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-synecdoche]])

## l1rn
“doing violence to others in order to get money”

# 22:18
## h7h1
In verse 18, Yahweh stops speaking directly to Jehoiakim and begins to speak to other people. Here “lament” refers to grieving at Jehoiakim’s death. Alternate translation: “They will not lament for Jehoiakim when he dies” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## n6j5
Yahweh uses the word “Woe” several times for emphasis. He addresses the various people who would normally show great sadness when someone dies. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 22:19
## s435
The burial of Jehoiakim is spoken of as being the same as the manner in which people would bury a donkey. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “They will bury his dead body the way they would bury a dead donkey; they will drag it away and throw it out” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 22:20
## ggs2
See [[JER-ggs2]]
## gv51
This idiom refers to shouting. In this case, they are crying out in grief. Alternate translation: “Cry out” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## ptb6
a mountain range that was southeast of Jerusalem (See: [[translate-names]])

# 22:21
## t5yr
“I spoke to you when you were doing well”

## fs7b
“This was your way of life”

## n4fz
Listening is a metonym for obeying. Alternate translation: “you have not obeyed me” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 22:22
## l2p1
This is a wordplay—Yahweh uses the idea of “shepherd” in two different ways. Here “shepherds” is a metaphor for the leaders of Jerusalem, and the wind “leads” them away. The wind represents Yahweh. Alternate translation: “I will take your leaders away as if the wind had blown them away” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## m6ua
“become captives” or “go into exile”

# 22:23
## i6fc
Yahweh speaks of the royal palace as “Lebanon” and “cedar buildings” because it was constructed with a lot of cedar. Alternate translation: “You who live in a palace made from the cedars of Lebanon” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## n9dg
This “You” is singular and refers to the king. (See: [[figs-you]])

## x4j2
The meaning of the Hebrew is not clear. This can be stated in active form. Possible meanings are (1) “they will greatly pity you” or (2) “you will groan greatly.” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## vfv9
The pain that the king will feel when his enemies defeat him will be as intense as the pain a woman feels in childbirth. Alternate translation: “when you experience pain as intense as the pain of a woman in childbirth” (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 22:24
## lcv6
Yahweh speaks to Jehoiachin as if it were possible for him to be the ring on Yahweh’s hand. (See: [[figs-hypo]])

## fu3p
A signet ring was used by a king to place his seal of authority on documents. The signet ring therefore represents authority to rule. The right hand also represents authority to rule. Alternate translation: “the signet ring on my right hand that represented my authority to be king” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## dv7c
“I would tear you off from my hand” or “I would quickly pull you off of my hand”

# 22:25
## s6ia
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I have made it possible for the ones seeking your life to capture you” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## h93i
Here the metonym “hand” represents power and control. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## sr7r
This phrase represents wanting or trying to kill someone. See how this phrase is translated in [Jeremiah 11:21](../11/21.md). Alternate translation: “trying to kill you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:27
## cm6e
This refers to the land of Judah.

# 22:28
## clg1
It is not clear who is speaking in verse 28.

## h3sy
Jehoiachin is spoken of as a pot that was completely worthless and not liked by anyone. The questions emphasize that he has no value or friends. Alternate translation: “Jehoiachin is as useless as a broken vessel and no one is happy with him.” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

## qal1
This rhetorical question explains what will happen to the worthless Jehoiachin. Alternate translation: “Therefore the enemy will remove Jehoiachin and his family from the land and take them to a foreign land.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## vh5v
Jehoiachin and his descendants are spoke of as if they had been poured out in another country like garbage from a vessel. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 22:29
## ac8x
Yahweh speaks his message to all the people of the land by calling out to the land where they live. The title is repeated to call attention to the message. (See: [[figs-apostrophe]])

# 22:30
## ll6p
Jehoiachin has several sons. This phrase, then, means it is as if he was childless. His children will not succeed him as king. Alternate translation: “It will be as if he did not have any children” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## h57d
Sitting on a throne is a metonym for ruling as king. Alternate translation: “become king” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 23:intro
## u767
See [[JER-u767]]
# 23:1
## q9nk
See [[JER-q9nk]]
## z9sb
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 23:2
## hus7
These two phrases have similar meanings. The second one strengthens the thought in the first. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 23:3
## rk1f
See [[JER-rk1f]]
## n6wn
Yahweh refers to the land of Israel as if it were good pasture for his people. This good pasture is a metonym for provision for their needs. Alternate translation: “to good pasture” or “to where all their needs will be supplied” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## k44c
The word “increase” explains how they will be “fruitful.” Alternate translation: “they will increase greatly in number” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 23:4
## ybw9
“I will appoint”

## t983
The words “be shattered” mean that someone has caused them to be afraid and mean basically the same thing as “fear.” This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “no one will make them afraid anymore” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## n8p6
“None of my people”

## ku28
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 23:5
## xn4x
This word alerts us to pay attention to the surprising information that follows.

## wru3
This idiom means he will appoint a successor. (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## wi4u
This future king descended from David is spoken of as if he were a branch grown on a tree. Alternate translation: “a righteous descendant” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## he9a
Some modern versions translate this as “a legitimate successor to the throne.”

## ki8r
The abstract nouns “justice” and “righteousness” can be stated as actions. Alternate translation: “cause people to act justly and rightly” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## k63x
Here “land” refers to the people who live in the land. Alternate translation: “for all the people in the nation” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 23:6
## ls7f
Here “Judah” refers to the people of Judah. Alternate translation: “the people of Judah will be rescued” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## cl3x
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “He will rescue Judah from their enemies” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## y7s1
Here “Israel” refers to the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “the people of Israel will live in security” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## b9n9
this refers to the king

## tg6a
The abstract noun “righteousness” can be stated as an action. Alternate translation: “Yahweh, the one who does what is right for us” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 23:7
## kss4
See [[JER-kss4]]
## t4jx
The word “see” here alerts the reader to pay attention to the important information that follows.

## xb5a
“as surely as Yahweh is alive.” The people use this expression to show that what they say next is certainly true. This is a way of making a solemn promise. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 4:2](../04/02.md). Alternate translation: “I solemnly swear” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 23:8
## d1ds
See [[JER-d1ds]]
## yqm5
These two phrases mean the same thing and can be combined. Alternate translation: “who brought back” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## e2wl
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house. In this case it refers to the kingdom of Israel. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 3:18](../03/18.md). Alternate translation: “Israel” or “the kingdom of Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## eqs7
This refers to the way the northern ten tribes of Israel were captured and spread out among the surrounding countries.

## c7iz
This refers to Israel, from where they were scattered. This can be stated explicitly. Alternate translation: “Then they will live in their own land again” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 23:9
## j7qj
See [[JER-j7qj]]
## f11c
The prophet speaks of his heart being broken and his bones trembling because he is afraid of the judgment that will result from the lies of the false prophets. Alternate translation: “I have great fear for what will happen because of the false prophets” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## lb5c
This idiom refers to deep sadness. Alternate translation: “I am very sad” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## g6mk
Here trembling is associated with fear. Alternate translation: “I am very afraid” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## a63q
People who are drunk are not able to control themselves. Likewise, Jeremiah has lost control of himself out of fear of Yahweh’s punishment. Alternate translation: “I am like a drunk man; I cannot control myself” (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 23:10
## v7jt
The land is spoken of as if it were a container and the adulterers were objects that filled the container completely. That is, everyone in the land is an adulterer, which in turn is an exaggeration for very many people being adulterers. (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

## pz91
This word here probably has two senses. It has the literal meaning that the men in the nation had committed adultery against their own wives, and it also has the figurative meaning that they had abandoned Yahweh in order to worship idols. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## t6w1
Some modern versions interpret this Hebrew phrase as “the land mourns.”

## u1vx
The wicked actions of the false prophets are spoken of as if they were walking on an evil path. Alternate translation: “These prophets are doing things that are wicked” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 23:11
## g5xv
See [[JER-g5xv]]
## pq1v
The prophets and priests are defiled by sin in the same way that water can be polluted by filth. Alternate translation: “the prophets and the priests are sinful” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 23:12
## fgb1
The danger of their actions is spoken of as if they were about to fall off the slippery edge of a cliff in the dark and injure themselves. Alternate translation: “there actions are unstable and dangerous, like walking on a slippery place in the darkness” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## cdv6
Yahweh speaks of disaster as an enemy that he will send to attack the priests and false prophets. Alternate translation: “I will cause them to experience disaster” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 23:13
## q2w8
See [[JER-q2w8]]
## sqi5
The deception of the people by the false prophets is spoken of as leading them down the wrong path. Alternate translation: “deceived my people, the Israelites” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 23:14
## kpq8
Possible meanings are (1) they were literally unfaithful to their wives or (2) their worship of other gods is spoken of as spiritual adultery.

## gu9j
This idiom means they lived a life of deceit. Alternate translation: “live in dishonesty” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## v93v
Here “hands” refers to the whole person. Alternate translation: “They strengthen those who do evil” or “They encourage people who do evil” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## hf5u
This can be state in positive form. Alternate translation: “everyone continues in their sin”

## cc8r
Sodom and Gomorrah were cities that were so wicked that God destroyed them with fire from heaven. Alternate translation: “They all became as evil as Sodom … as wicked as Gomorrah” (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 23:15
## t5fw
Jeremiah often uses these words to introduce an important message from Yahweh. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 6:6](../06/06.md).

## cv5x
The word “look” here alerts the reader to pay attention to the important information that follows.

## ug5g
Wormwood is a plant that is very bitter and unpleasant to eat. This phrase describes the punishment that Yahweh was going to bring about on the evil prophets. In the same way wormwood was bitter and the water was poisonous, so would Yaweh’s judgment be on the evil prophets. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## wjd4
“I will soon”

## zxk6
Here the evil teaching of the priests and false prophets is spoken of as if it were something filthy that ruined the land. Alternate translation: “evil has come from the prophets” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## fxa4
dirty and unsafe things that spoil the air, water, or land where they are found

# 23:16
## z212
See [[JER-z212]]
## u2nu
Jeremiah often uses these words to introduce an important message from Yahweh. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 6:6](../06/06.md).

## yu7d
“The prophets have caused you to believe something that is not true!”

## qzm5
Here “minds” refers to the thoughts of the false prophets. Alternate translation: “visions that they imagined” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## nij1
Here “mouth” refers to what Yahweh said. Alternate translation: “not what Yahweh said” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 23:17
## e3yb
The word “constantly” is an exaggeration to emphasize that this is what they normally say. (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

## kg1k
Here “heart” refers to their desires. To follow those desires is spoken of as walking in them. Alternate translation: “everyone stubbornly does whatever they desire to do” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## ww1a
“Nothing bad will happen to you”

# 23:18
## rtl1
These questions are used to rebuke the priests and false prophets for not obeying Yahweh. Alternate translation: “No one advises Yahweh. No one understands what Yahweh says. No one obeys Yahweh’s commands.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## crs3
These two phrases mean the same thing. They can be combined in one phrase. Alternate translation: “obeys his word at all”

# 23:19
## sv8q
See [[JER-sv8q]]
## b4tl
These three phrases all refer to a great storm that is a metaphor for Yahweh’s anger. This can be stated as a simile. Alternate translation: “Yahweh’s anger is coming like a great storm, going out with fury and whirling like a tempest” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-simile]])

## gsy3
a violent windstorm

## w4qs
Yahweh’s anger is spoken of as if it were a windstorm whirling around the wicked. Alternate translation: “It is coming upon the wicked like a windstorm” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-simile]])

# 23:20
## n8pg
Yahweh’s wrath is spoken of as if it were alive and could act on its own. Alternate translation: “Yahweh will not stop being angry until he” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## p9sd
Here “heart’s intentions” refers to the things that Yahweh wanted to happen. Alternate translation: “completed and accomplished all the punishment that he had planned” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## b1p2

## e2zq

## jy71

# 23:22
## nx49
Yahweh states what would have happened if the priests and false prophets had participated in Yahweh’s council meeting in heaven. This hypothetical situation is impossible. Alternate translation: “if they had truly listened to me” (See: [[figs-hypo]])

## kym3
In ancient times, God was thought to hold meetings with the angels in heaven. Yahweh is referring to this kind of meeting, which humans could not attend. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 23:18](../23/18.md).

## n2ix
This idiom means they would stop doing these things. (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 25:6
## yxs7
Jeremiah speaks of a person being devoted to a god as if the person were walking behind the god. Alternate translation: “do not become devoted to other gods” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## cgg6
The word “him” refers to Yahweh. Possible meanings for the phrase “the work of your hands” are (1) it is a reference to the idols that the people have made with their hands. Alternate translation: “do not provoke Yahweh with the idols that you have made” or (2) It is an idiom that refers to the actions of a person, with the word “hands” being a synecdoche that represents the person who does those actions. Alternate translation: “do not provoke Yahweh by the things that you do” (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 25:7
## fz11
See how you translated a similar phrase in [Jeremiah 25:6](../25/06.md). Possible meanings for the phrase “the work of your hands” are (1) it is a reference to the idols that the people have made with their hands. Alternate translation: “you have provoked me to harm you because of the idols that you have made with your hands” or (2) It is an idiom that refers to the actions of a person, with the word “hands” being a synecdoche that represents the person who does those actions. Alternate translation: “you have provoked me to harm you because of the things that you do” (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 25:34
## i7rd

## tec2

## ufa1

## chn4
Usually it is the sheep that are slaughtered, but here Yahweh says that he will slaughter the shepherds. The word “slaughter” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “for the time has come for me to slaughter you” (See: [[figs-irony]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## vka9
Yahweh had destroyed a place of worship at Shiloh, and he was threatening to destroy this place of worship. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## qd3h
What Yahweh would turn the city into is a metonym for what people would use the city for. Alternate translation: “I will make this city so that people will ask me to destroy others the way I have destroyed this city” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## w3q2
“so that all the nations on earth can see me do it”

## id3z

## zr32
Possible meanings are (1) The people chose to believe the lie of false peace and did not want to be confronted with the truth, or (2) the people believed the other prophets proclaiming peace and saw Jeremiah as a false prophet who should be stoned for leading the people astray.

## u7ib

## u76z
The voice is a synecdoche for the person, and the same word can be translated “listen” or “obey.” Alternate translation: “obey Yahweh” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 26:14
## kr6k
The words “good” and “right” mean the same thing. The eye is a metonym for the person’s thoughts. Alternate translation: “Do to me whatever you think is the right thing to do” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 26:15
## r93l
Blood is a metonym for violent death, and to bring blood on someone is to make them guilty of violent death. Alternate translation: “you are making yourselves and this city and its inhabitants guilty of the violent death of an innocent person” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## cu9w
These are two ways of speaking of the same people, perhaps first as a group and then as individuals. The city is a metonym for the people in the city. Alternate translation: “all the people in the city and every one of the people who live here” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## mi1q
The ear is a metonym for what the ear hears. Alternate translation: “for you to hear” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 26:16
## jf98
The person’s name is a metonym for his authority. Alternate translation: “with the authority of Yahweh our God” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## z8nb

## t6dn

## az6u
Jeremiah often uses these words to introduce an important message from Yahweh. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 6:6](../06/06.md).

## pi1j
“Zion” and “the hill of the temple” refer to the same place. When a farmer plows a field, he turns over all the dirt and uproots all the plants that are growing there. A thicket is so full of bushes that no one can use it for anything. These two metaphors cannot be literally true at the same time, but they emphasize that Yahweh will allow the invaders to completely destroy the temple area. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## qp3q
The speakers are trying to get the hearers to agree with what they are saying. Alternate translation: “You know very well that Hezekiah … did not put him to death.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## pa6c

## vxq8
The speakers are trying to get the hearers to agree with what they are saying. Alternate translation: “You know very well that he feared Yahweh and appeased the face of Yahweh so that Yahweh … them.”

## gxw9

## fc9w
The speakers are trying to get the hearers to agree with what they are saying. The word “lives” is a metonym for the people. Alternate translation: “If we kill Jeremiah, we will do greater evil against our own lives.” or “We do not want to do worse things to ourselves.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

“I will cause things to go well for my people, Israel and Judah, again” or “I will cause my people, Israel and Judah, to live well again.” See how you translated similar words in [Jeremiah 29:14](../29/14.md).

# 30:5
## l2xq
Possible meanings are (1) that these are the words of Yahweh referring to himself as “we.” Alternate translation: “I have heard” or (2) that these are the words of the people of Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “You people of Jerusalem say, ‘We have heard”

## mej1
Possible meanings are (1) “people cry out in dread because there is no peace” or (2) “you cry out in dread because there is no peace.”

# 30:6
## br85
“You know that no man has ever given birth to a child”

## t7ik
A woman about to give birth is in great pain and unable to do anything. The young men are so frightened that their bodies hurt, and they are unable to fight. Alternate translation: “The young men are holding their bellies like a woman giving birth; they all look sick because they are so afraid.” (See: [[figs-simile]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

## uva8

## zir5
Jacob’s name is a metonym for his descendants. Yahweh speaks to Jacob as if he were speaking to another person. You may need to make explicit to where Jacob will return. ## v4ha
Yahweh describes the people of Israel as an unfaithful wife who takes lovers other than her husband. Here “lovers” refers to other nations. The Israelites allied with them and worshiped their gods instead of relying on Yahweh. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 30:15
## q12y
Yahweh asks a question here to make the people think about why they are asking him for help now. Alternate translation: “You are calling for help for your injury because you have disobeyed me.” or “Do not call for help for your injury.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 30:16
## d7ma
Destroying the nation is spoken of as consuming or eating it. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “So those who destroy you, their enemies will destroy them” or “So I will destroy everyone who destroys you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## a2ze
To plunder is to steal from peaceful people using violence, and plunder is what people steal.

## i8dj
To despoil here is to take items from an enemy one has defeated, and the items one takes are the spoil.

An outcast is a person whom other people do not accept or allow to associate with them. Alternate translation: “they called you: Rejected” or “they said, ‘Nobody wants you’”

# 31:2
## ht7e
The phrase “found favor” is an idiom. Alternate translation: “While the people who have survived the sword have been in the wilderness, I have had grace on them” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## ml2j

# 31:3
## egs4
Jeremiah speaks of himself as if he were the people of Israel. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## rmr8
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be stated as “faithful” or “faithfully.” Alternate translation: “I have been faithful to my covenant and brought you near to me” or “I have faithfully loved you and brought you near to me” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 31:4
## ab77
See [[JER-ab77]]
## bv1q
Yahweh purposefully repeats the idea that he will build Israel to emphasize it. If your language can only use active verbs and has some other way to emphasize the idea, you may want to use it here. Alternate translation: “I will build you up again. Remember this: I will build you” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-parallelism]])

## bt11
It was common to refer to nations as if they were women. However, “virgin” makes a person think about a young woman who has never married and so has never had the opportunity to be faithless to her husband. Therefore, calling Israel a virgin is an ironic use of language. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 18:13](../18/13.md). Alternate translation: “Israel, who falsely pretends to be fully devoted to me” or “my faithless people Israel” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-irony]])

## v1aw
musical instruments with heads like a drum that can be hit and with pieces of metal around their sides that sound when the instruments are shaken (See: [[translate-unknown]])

## z57h

## n8v4

## dc3t

## pyt3
A shepherd cares for and protects his sheep, and Yahweh is promising to care for and protect the Israelites. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 31:11
## t3xt
These two phrases mean basically the same thing and emphasize that it is Yahweh who has rescued the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “For Yahweh has rescued the people of Israel from their enemy who was too strong for them” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 31:12
## x6vs
“on Zion, the high place” or “on Mount Zion.” Being on top of a hill is a metaphor for being happy. If your language associates mountain tops with sadness, it would be best to leave out the metaphor. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## s2y2
This means they will be strong and healthy, and they will prosper. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## d4iw
The world “never” is a generalization. The Israelites will almost always feel joy. (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 31:13
## f4jc

## z18g
“Yahweh will change”

# 31:14
## v9rm
The abstract noun “abundance” can be translated as “many good things.” The lives of the priests are spoken of as if they were a field that would become completely wet when much rain falls on it. Life is a metonym for the person. Alternate translation: “I will give the priests all the good things they would like” or “I will satisfy the priests with good things” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 31:15
## gfw6
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “I hear a voice in Ramah” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## qr6k
Rachel was the wife of Jacob/Israel and was the mother of the tribes of Joseph and Benjamin. Her name is a metonym for the women of Israel who are crying because the Babylonians have killed their children or taken them away. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## iel2
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “She will not let anyone comfort her, for her children are dead” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-explicit]])

# 31:16
## sw6x
The voice and eyes are spoken of as if they were people whom Rachel needed to keep from moving. Alternate translation: “Stop weeping aloud and stop crying tears” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## m4pv
Yahweh is speaking to the Israelites as if he were speaking to Rachel ([Jeremiah 31:15](../31/15.md)), so all instances of “your” and the commands are singular. (See: [[figs-you]] and [[figs-apostrophe]])

## n1bx

## v8ly
Yahweh is speaking to the Israelites as if he were speaking to Rachel ([Jeremiah 31:15](../31/15.md)), so all instances of “your” and the commands are singular. (See: [[figs-you]] and [[figs-apostrophe]])

# 31:18
## we7q
Ephraim was the ancestor of the largest tribe in Israel. His name is a metonym for the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “I have certainly heard the descendants of Ephraim sorrowing” or “I have certainly heard the people of Israel sorrowing” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## mfd1
The speaker repeats the phrase to show either that Yahweh punished him severely or that Ephraim learned what Yahweh wanted to teach him through punishing him. The words “I have been punished” can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “You punished me. Yes, you punished me severely” or “You punished me, and I learned from you when you punished me” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## s9qf
When people train calves, they strike them and otherwise cause them to suffer. Yahweh had caused the people of Israel to suffer. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## pay9
Another possible meaning is “disciplined.”

# 31:19
## ip4s
in grief. If people use a different phrase or action in your language to show that they are very sad, you may want to use it here. Alternate translation: “I rubbed my brow” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[translate-symaction]])

## p5xj
The words “ashamed” and “humiliated” mean basically the same thing and intensify the idea of shame. Alternate translation: “I was completely ashamed” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 31:20
## lm82
Yahweh is speaking tenderly, trying to comfort the people. These questions can be translated as statements. Alternate translation: “Ephraim is my precious child. He is my dear, delightful son. (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 31:24
## w96e
The name of the man Judah is a metonym for the land on which his descendants, the people of the tribe of Judah, live, and the cities of Judah are spoken of as if they were the family of the man Judah, all of them living “there,” in the land of Judah. Alternate translation: “it will be as if the land were a house in which Judah lived with his family” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## u22q
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## h4zz
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house, in this case the people of Israel and the people of Judah. The house in turn is spoken of as if it were a field in which Yahweh would sow seed, and the people and the animals they raise for food, milk, and skins are spoken of as if they are that seed. Alternate translation: “I will cause the people of Israel and Judah to become many people with many animals” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## gu9q
The city is spoken of as if it were a plant that someone could pull out of the ground or a building that someone could break down. This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will not allow anyone to pull up or overthrow the city again” or “No one will tear the city down or destroy it again” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## xs6y

## mul1
“after Zedekiah had been king of Judah for more than nine years and Nebuchadnezzar had been king for more than seventeen years”

## nkx2
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “they were keeping Jeremiah as a prisoner” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## fp96

## ke5d
This was an open area attached to the king’s palace that was surrounded by buildings and in which they kept prisoners.

# 32:3
## ih69
It might be best to translate so that the reader understands that other people helped Zedekiah do this. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## p2a2
Here “him” refers to Jeremiah. It is not clear why Jeremiah is referring to himself by name here. You may translate using first person, as the UST does. (See: [[figs-123person]])

## x9qt
Zekekiah uses a question to rebuke Jeremiah. Alternate translation: “It is wrong for you to continue prophesying and saying” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## sd28
Yahweh speaks of the city as if it were a small object that one person could give to another. The word “hand” is a metonym for the power or control that the hand exercises. Alternate translation: “I am about to put this city under the control of the king of Babylon” or “I am about to allow the king of Babylon to do whatever he wants with this city” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## r41w
It might be best to translate so that the reader understands that other people would help the king of Babylon take control of the city.

# 32:4
## ymp7
The word “hand” is a metonym for the power or control that the hand exercises. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will certainly put him under the control of the king of Babylon” or “I will certainly allow the king of Babylon to do whatever he wants with him” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## jf8a
Here “mouth” and “eyes” represent the whole person. Alternate translation: “Zedekiah himself will see and directly speak with Nebuchadnezzar” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

Here “you” is plural and refers to the people in Jerusalem. (See: [[figs-you]])

## bkt2
It is not clear why Jeremiah is referring to himself by name here. You may translate using first person, as the UST does. (See: [[figs-123person]])

## d1sv
The long quotation that begins with the words “The word of Yahweh” in verse 6 ends here. You may translate this as indirect speech. Alternate translation: “Jeremiah said that the word of Yahweh had come to him and told him that Hanamel son of Shallum his uncle was coming to him and would tell him to buy his uncle’s field that was in Anathoth for himself, for the right to buy it belonged to Jeremiah.” (See: [[figs-quotations]])

## puz1
This means the sealed scroll and the unsealed scroll.

# 32:17
## xs6m
The word “woe” here indicates that in this prayer Jeremiah is sad, complaining or lamenting. The word “look” here indicates that the next words are an introduction or background for the most important part of the prayer. (See: [[writing-background]])

## r8xl
The phrase “raised arm” is a metonym for the strength of the arm, so the words “your great strength” and “your raised arm” form a doublet. Alternate translation: “by your great power” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-doublet]])

# 32:18
## zsm3
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be stated as “faithful” or “faithfully.” Alternate translation: “You are faithful to your covenant towards thousands” or “You keep your promise and faithfully love thousands” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## cd54
The word “guilt” is a metonym for Yahweh punishing people because they are guilty of doing evil things. Yahweh punishing people is spoken of as if he were pouring a large container full of liquid or small objects into people’s laps as they sit. Alternate translation: “you punish children for the sins of their parents” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 32:19
## z67p
See [[JER-z67p]]
## zh7i
Open eyes are a metonym for what the person sees. How a person lives is spoken of as if he were walking on a path. Alternate translation: “You see everything that people do” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## jhc8
The abstract nouns “conduct” and “deeds,” which are similar in meaning, can be translated as verbs. Alternate translation: “and will reward everyone according to how good or evil the things they do are” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-doublet]])

# 32:20
## ff83
This refers to an event in the past when God used his power to free the people of Israel from slavery in Egypt.

# 32:21
## yz8j
The phrase “strong hand” is a metonym for strength, and the phrase “raised arm” is a metonym for the strength of the arm, so the phrases “strong hand” and “raised arm” form a doublet. Alternate translation: “by your great strength” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-doublet]])

## hz5q
who creates things so he can make them exist forever

# 33:4
## g9p1
This can be translated in active form. Possible meanings are (1) the people tore the houses down to make walls to defend against the Chaldeans. Alternate translation: “that the people have torn down to defend against the siege ramps and the sword” or (2) Alternate translation: “the houses that the Chaldeans have torn down to make siege ramps so they can make war” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## u2ec
This is a metonym for people dying violently when soldiers kill with swords. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 33:5
## lp8r
The words “wrath” and “fury” mean basically the same thing and emphasize the intensity of his wrath. Alternate translation: “in my extreme wrath” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## czm7
Yahweh is represented by his “face.” This phrase shows Yahweh’s displeasure with the city. Alternate translation: “I have turned away from you” or “I am no longer concerned with you” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 33:7
## z3sl
