# 33:9
## l5i7
The city is a metonym for the people who live in the city. Alternate translation: “the people who live in this city … do for the people who live there … give to the people who live there” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## t8ug
The word “song” is a metonym for the object about which people will sing the song. Alternate translation: “something about which all the people groups of the earth will sing songs of praise and honor to me, Yahweh” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## l75s
The hendiadys “fear and tremble” can be translated by a single verb. Alternate translation: “they will tremble with fear” (See: [[figs-hendiadys]])

## i3fz

## vmq7
The abstract noun “peace” can be translated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “because of all the good things that I will give to it and because I will cause it to be peaceful” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 33:21
## u6sr
The hypothetical statement that begins with the words “If you can break my covenant … times,” in verse 20 ends here. No one can change day and night, and no one can change God’s covenant with David. Alternate translation: “You know that you cannot break my covenant … times, so you know that you will never be able to break my covenant with David my servant” (See: [[figs-hypo]])

## xip8

## ufu8

## liq9

## wv8q

“What they are really saying is that my people are worthless and that my people will never again be a nation.”

## x6zy
The hypothetical statement that begins with the words “If I have not established the covenant” in verse 25 ends here. (See: [[figs-hypo]])

## w9mt
The hypothetical statement that begins with the words “If I have not established the covenant” in verse 25 ends here. This is a situation that Yahweh never expects to be true. Alternate translation: “I have established the covenant … so I will never reject … and I will bring” (See: [[figs-hypo]])

## z156
“I will cause things to go well for them again” or “I will cause them to live well again” See how similar words are translated in [Jeremiah 29:14](../29/14.md).

## e4m9
Translate as “have compassion on” is translated in ([Jeremiah 30:18](../30/18.md)).

This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “whom they had rescued at Gibeon” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## h1f8
This is a way to refer to people from every social status. Here “least” and “greatest” represent those who are least important and those who are most important. Alternate translation: “all the people from every social status” or “all the people, including the least important and the most important people” (See: [[figs-merism]])

## wx9l
Most likely not every individual in the town went. The word “all” is a generalization that means very many people went. Alternate translation: “many other people” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

## te2b

## yl1b
“Pay attention to what I am about to tell you”

## v54c

## tze5

## c9v6

# 42:10
## m39t
Yahweh refers to the people of Israel like a wall that can be built or torn down. Alternate translation: “I will cause you to prosper and I will not destroy you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## aj8z
Yahweh uses another example to express how he will cause the people of Israel to prosper and not be destroyed. He refers to them like a plant in the same way he referred to them as a wall. (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-parallelism]])

## a8zc
A disaster is here spoken of as something that a person can put onto someone else. Alternate translation: “I will prevent the disaster that I have caused to happen to you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 42:11
## k5t5
The words “save” and “rescue” mean the same thing and emphasize that Yahweh will, indeed, save. Alternate translation: “to save you completely” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## jv59
Here the word “hand” refers to power and authority. Alternate translation: “rescue you from his power” or “rescue you from him” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 42:13
## qi8z

## n28p

## t7ch

## pea7
The Israelites experiencing famine even in Egypt is spoken of as if famine would chase after them. Alternate translation: “You worry about famine in Israel but if you go to Egypt you will suffer from famine there” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## ss3l
The words “wrath” and “fury” mean basically the same thing. Together they emphasize the intensity of his wrath. Alternate translation: “my terrible wrath” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## rm4e
Yahweh punishing people because he is very angry is spoken of as if fury were a liquid that he pours out on the people. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will became very angry and punish you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## m8xv
All of these statements mean basically the same thing and emphasize how badly the nations will treat the people of Judah after Yahweh punishes them. Alternate translation: “People will be terrified when they see what has happened to you. They will curse and mock you” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## a86s

# 44:4
## jwn6
The word “I” refers to Yahweh.

# 44:5
## a6zd

# 44:6
## kck1
Becoming angry and punishing people is spoken of as if fury and wrath were liquids there could be poured on people. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I became very angry and punished them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## gje9
The words “fury” and “wrath” mean basically the same thing and emphasize the intensity of his wrath. Alternate translation: “my terrible wrath” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## if3y
Yahweh becoming angry and punishing the people of Judah is spoken of as his fury and wrath were a fire. Alternate translation: “my fury and wrath were like a fire” or “my punishment was like a fire” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## j69q
The words “ruins” and “devastations” mean basically the same thing. Together they emphasize the complete devastation of Judah and Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “they became completely devastated” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## bb1y
This refers to the current time at which Yahweh was speaking this message.

# 44:7
## gez9
Yahweh uses these questions to rebuke the people for doing things that cause him to punish them. Alternate translation: “You are doing … yourselves. You are causing … and babies.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## mnr5
The abstract noun “wickedness” can be stated as “wicked.” Alternate translation: “Why are you doing these wicked things that harm yourselves?” or “You are doing wicked things that cause terrible things to happen to you.” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## n4fj
Being separated from the people of Judah is spoken of as if the people are cut off from Judah the way a person may cut off a branch from a vine or cut off a piece of cloth. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Why are you causing me to remove you from the people of Judah … and babies?” or “You are causing me to remove you from the people of Judah … and babies.” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 44:8
## prs6
The abstract noun “wickedness” represents actions that are “wicked.” Here “hand” represents the whole person. Alternate translation: “You have offended me with the wicked things you have done” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-synecdoche]])

## bqw9
“The result of going to Egypt is that … and that”

# 44:9
## zk7z
Yahweh rebukes the people for not thinking about the wicked behavior of their ancestors and how Yahweh punished them. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Think about what happened to your ancestors and the kings of Judah and their wives when they did wicked things.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## v2a3
Yahweh rebukes the people for not thinking about their own wicked behavior and how Yahweh punished them. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Think about what happened to you and your wives when you did evil things in the land Judah and the streets of Jerusalem.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## w74z
Jerusalem is referred to by the part of the city where people walk. This means the people did evil things in public places. Alternate translation: “Jerusalem” or “the public places of Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 44:10
## ai8e

## a4hl

## sf5s
Here “hand” represents power or control. And, Nebuchadnezzar represents his whole army. The phrase “sought his life” is an idiom that means to want to kill someone. Alternate translation: “when I allowed the army of Nebuchadnezzar to defeat Zedekiah king of Judah” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 45:intro
## m5sy
See [[JER-m5sy]]
# 45:1
## n8gv
This information can be placed at the beginning of 45:1 as in the UST.

## uid7
The words “of the rule” are understood. Alternate translation: “in the fourth year of the rule of Jehoiakim” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## d24t
The word “fourth” is the ordinal form of the number four. (See: [[translate-ordinal]])

## w799
“Jeremiah said to Baruch”

# 45:3
## vg45
“for I was already suffering and now Yahweh causes me to be sad as well”

## jwx7
The abstract noun “groaning” can be stated as “cry.” Alternate translation: “I am tired because I cry so much” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 45:4
## sdw1
Yahweh is telling Jeremiah what to say to Baruch.

# 45:5
## jtj1
Yahweh uses a question to state that he knows Baruch is hoping for others to treat him in a special way. Alternate translation: “I know you hope for great things for yourself.” or “I know you hope others will honor you.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## m1dh
“For understand” or “Be aware”

## n1jc
Allowing Baruch to escape and remain alive is spoken of as if his life were plunder that he would take after a battle. Alternate translation: “but wherever you go, I will protect you and allow you to live” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## a5tu
things stolen from a place by force

# 46:intro
## ul65
See [[JER-ul65]]
# 46:1
## sfw3
See [[JER-sfw3]]
## e496
This idiom is used to introduce a special message from God. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 14:1](../14/01.md), and make any changes as needed. Alternate translation: “This is the message that Yahweh gave to Jeremiah” or “This is the message that Yahweh spoke to Jeremiah” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 46:2
## q4ql
This phrase indicates this message is intended for the nation of Egypt.

## zfd4
This is the name of a man. (See: [[translate-names]])

## dv75
This is the name of a city on the west bank of the Euphrates. (See: [[translate-names]])

## tsq6
Here Nebuchadnezzar represents his army. Alternate translation: “that the army of Nebuchadnezzar king of Babylon” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## nsv9
This refers to the fourth year of Jehoiakim’s reign. This can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “in the fourth year of the reign of Jehoiakim” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## v9gg
The word “fourth” is the ordinal form of “four.” (See: [[translate-ordinal]])

# 46:4
## vhi9
A “harness” is a set of straps placed on a horse so that it can pull a chariot. Here “mount up on the horses” means that the soldiers rode in the chariots that the horses were pulling.

## z233
A helmet is armor which protects the head in battle.

## v58m
This phrase means to “sharpen” the spear.

# 46:5
## h8b9
See [[JER-h8b9]]
## nj2q
The word “I” here refer to Yahweh. Yahweh uses a question to introduce the vision and to draw attention to what he says next. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Look at what is happening here” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## hry1
The abstract noun “terror” can be stated as “terrified.” Alternate translation: “The Egyptian soldiers are terrified” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 46:6
## gr1r
These two phrases share similar meanings and emphasize that no one, not even the strongest and fastest, can escape. The nominal adjective “the swift” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “even the swiftest soldier cannot escape” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-nominaladj]])

## bv4q
Both statements mean the same thing. Being and dying in battle is spoken of as if the soldiers stumble and fall. Alternate translation: “The Egyptian soldiers suffer defeat and die in the north by the Euphrates River” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 46:7
## qv9y
See [[JER-qv9y]]
## j7fk
Yahweh uses a question to draw attention to what he is about to say concerning Egypt. This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Look at who rises like the Nile … the rivers.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 46:8
## n8ng
The Nile river floods its banks once a year, covering the surrounding area with water. Yahweh compares the kingdom of Egypt to the Nile because the Egyptian people think they are mighty enough to destroy cities in all the lands. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## udt1
Here “Egypt” represents all the people of Egypt. Alternate translation: “The Egyptians rise … The Egyptians say … We will” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 46:9
## ew5h
Here “horses” and “chariots” represent the soldiers who use horses and chariots in battle. The leaders of Egypt are ordering their soldiers to start the battle. Alternate translation: “Go up to battle, you soldiers on horses. Fight furiously, you soldiers in chariots” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## a538
This command can be addressed directly to the soldiers. What they were to go out for can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “You soldiers from Cush and Put, who are skillful with your shields, go out to battle” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## fz8w
These are biblical names for the countries of Ethiopia and Libya. (See: [[translate-names]])

## c8vs
The ideas of soldiers and going out to battle are understood from earlier in the sentence. They can be repeated. This command can also be addressed directly to the soldiers. Alternate translation: “you soldiers from Lud, who are skillful at bending your bows, go out to battle” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## mem7
This is the name of the people group from the nation of Lud. Lud is the biblical name for Lydia. (See: [[translate-names]])

## awb5
The phrase “bend the bow” means to pull the string of the bow back with an arrow in order to shoot the arrow. Alternate translation: “skillful at shooting arrows from their bows” or “skillful with the bow and arrow” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 46:10
## b6mc
See [[JER-b6mc]]
## k221
This phrase refers to the day the Egyptians lose the battle to the Babylonians.

## kb95
Yahweh speaks of himself in third person. This can be stated in first person. Alternate translation: “for me, Lord Yahweh of hosts, and I will avenge myself on my foes” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## xmi3
This speaks of Yahweh’s sword as if it were devouring people and drinking blood. Both these phrases are saying similar thing and are combined to emphasize that there will be complete destruction. Alternate translation: “I will completely destroy my enemy. It will be like my sword devours them and gets drunk on their blood” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-personification]])

## c3fe
Yahweh punishing and killing his enemies is spoken of as if he would use a sword to kill them. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## i9dw
Yahweh avenging himself by causing the Egyptians to lose in battle against the Babylonians is spoken of as if the Egyptian army would be a sacrifice to Yahweh. Alternate translation: “For the Egyptians will be like a sacrifice” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 46:11
## h7iu
See [[JER-h7iu]]
## b1x3
Gilead was a region famous for having people skillful with medicine. Yahweh is mocking the Egyptians by telling them to go get medicine, but he knows it will not help them. (See: [[figs-irony]])

## k73x
The people of Egypt are spoken of as if they were the virgin daughter of Egypt. Alternate translation: “people of Egypt” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 46:12
## m6ni
This word means a condition of feeling ashamed or the loss of respect.

## xc2h
Here “earth” is a metonym for the people of the earth and a generalization for the people who know what has happened to Egypt. All the people of the earth hearing the Egyptians crying is spoken of as if their laments filled the earth. Alternate translation: “All the people of the earth hear you crying” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-hyperbole]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## jk5t
Soldiers dying in battle is spoken of as if they were stumbling and falling down. Alternate translation: “for your soldiers die in battle” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 46:13
## q31x
Here Nebuchadnezzar represents his army. Alternate translation: “when the army of Nebuchadnezzar king of Babylon” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## jr9a
Here “land” represents the people. Alternate translation: “the people of Egypt” or “the Egyptians” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 46:14
## g5lu
Translate the name of this city as you did in [Jeremiah 44:1](../44/01.md). (See: [[translate-names]])

## qy9a
Translate the names of these cities as you did in [Jeremiah 2:16](../02/16.md). (See: [[translate-names]])

## b4vr
The word “sword” here represents soldiers carrying weapons. The enemy soliders killing people is spoken of as if their swords were devouring people. Alternate translation: “your enemies will kill people all around you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 46:15
## a8tg
Some Bibles translate this as “Why has Apis fled? Why did your bull not stand?” Apis was an Egyptian god that was represented by the form of a bull. The bull is often a symbol for strength. That is why the ULT and other versions of the Bible translate this as “strong ones” or “mighty ones,” which refers to soldiers.

## i1pz
Yahweh uses a question to mock the strength of the Egyptian soldiers. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Your soldiers are face-down on the ground and will not stand” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 46:16
## a5tm
“Yahweh causes more and more of your soldiers to stumble”

## c7x1
Here “sword” represents the enemy soldiers who are carrying weapons. Alternate translation: “Let us run away from our enemies because they are killing us” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 46:17
## bd2w
Possible meanings are (1) “they” refers to people in general who are in Egypt or (2) “they” refers to the foreign soldiers who are fleeing to their native lands of Cush, Put, and Lud.

## kv5n
These two phrases are saying that the nation of Egypt has become of little importance. The phrase “only a noise” is an idiom that means a person says he will do something but he does not do it. Alternate translation: “Pharaoh brags much but cannot do what he brags he will” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 46:18
## a2ci
Here “King” refers to Yahweh. This can be stated in first person. Alternate translation: “this is what I, the King, declare” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## gmj6
This refers to the nation of Babylon who will be as overwhelming to the nation of Egypt as the these two mountains are to the plains that surround them. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## t2bs
This is the name of a mountain in the northern part of Israel. (See: [[translate-names]])

# 46:19
## n24i
“Prepare to go into exile”

# 46:20
## nf61
The nation of Egypt, which was very strong and prosperous, is spoken of as if it were a beautiful young cow. Alternate translation: “Egypt is like a very beautiful heifer” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## pcx7
The enemy army coming to attack the Egyptians is spoken of as if the army were an insect that stings. Alternate translation: “but a powerful army like a stinging insect” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 46:21
## duz9
The writer compares soldiers to “fattened bulls” because the soldiers are well cared for by the Egyptians just as a farmer takes care of a bull and makes it fat. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## y6gt
To “stand together” is an idiom meaning to remain united. The writer is saying that the soldiers will not fight as a unit but will run away thinking only of saving themselves. (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## h8zt
This speaks of a day as if it travels and arrives in a location. Alternate translation: “they will experience disaster on that day” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 46:22
## pc27
The inability of the Egyptians to do anything to stop their enemy is spoken of as if they were snakes that can only hiss and crawl away. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## z1yp
The enemies coming with the weapons to destroy the Egyptians is spoken of as if they were woodcutters with axes preparing to cut down a tree. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 46:23
## q15l
This continues speaking of the enemy army attacking the Egyptians as if the army were woodcutters cutting down trees. Alternate translation: “The enemy army will kill many Egyptians like woodcutters cutting down a forest … even though there are very many trees” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## p42g
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## gvh6
This is a type of insect that travels in very large groups and can cause great destruction by eating crops.

## dl76
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “no one will be able to count them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 46:24
## ut6g
The people of Egypt are spoken of as if they were the daughter of Egypt. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “The enemy army will humiliate the people of Egypt” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## n1ba
Here “hand” represents power or control. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I, Yahweh, will allow the people from the north to defeat the Egyptians” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 46:25
## v4iz
“Amon” is the king of the Egyptian gods. “Thebes” is the capital of the northern part of Egypt, and here it represents the people of Thebes. Alternate translation: “Amon, god of Thebes” or “Amon, god of the people of Thebes” (See: [[translate-names]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## au2w
Here “Egypt” represents the people. Alternate translation: “all the people of Egypt and the gods they worship” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 46:26
## pq89
Here “hand” represents power or control. The phrase “seeking their lives” is an idiom that mean to want to kill someone. Alternate translation: “I will allow those who are wanting to kill them to defeat them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## n18z
Here “hand” represents power or control. “Nebuchadnezzar … his servants” names specifically “the ones seeking their lives.” Alternate translation: “that is, I will allow Nebuchadnezzar king of Babylon and his servants to defeat the Egyptians” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## zb51
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Then after this, people will live in Egypt again” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 46:27
## cfi7
These two phrases mean the same thing. Here “Jacob” and “Israel” represent the people of Israel. Yahweh is emphasizing that the people should not be afraid. Alternate translation: “people of Israel, my servants, do not be afraid” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## n8yh
“from the land where they are captives”

# 46:28
## r2ij
The abstract noun “destruction” can be stated using the verb “destroy.” Alternate translation: “I will completely destroy all the nations” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## h92x
This can be stated in positive form. Alternate translation: “will certainly punish you” (See: [[figs-doublenegatives]])

# 47:1
## up7u
The idiom “the word of Yahweh that came to” is used to introduce a special message from God. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 14:1](../14/01.md), and make any changes as needed. Alternate translation: “This is the message that Yahweh gave to Jeremiah” or “This is the message that Yahweh spoke to Jeremiah” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## z4d3
The idiom “this word came to” is used to introduce a special message from God. Alternate translation: “Yahweh gave this message to Jeremiah” or “Yahweh spoke this message to Jeremiah” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## n7b3
Here “Pharaoh” represents the Egyptians army. Alternate translation: “before Pharaoh’s army” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 47:2
## br4s
The word “see” alerts us to pay attention to what is about to be said.

## na2f
The two phrases have a similar meaning. The enemy army is spoken of as if it were a flood. Alternate translation: “an army in the north will come like a flood. They will be powerful like an overflowing river” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## wnx1
This continues speaking of the army from the north as an overflowing river. Alternate translation: “like an overflowing river, the army from the north will destroy the land” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 47:3
## yv46
Together these represent the sounds of an oncoming army. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## yya7
These two phrase have similar meanings and they are combined to emphasize the loud noise they will make. Alternate translation: “at the very loud sound of the wheels of the chariots” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 47:4
## q4r1
This speaks of a day as if it were something that travels and arrives in a place. Alternate translation: “For on that day, the enemy army will devastate all of the Philistines and cut off from Tyre” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## f7br
Removing someone is spoken of as if they were cut off the way a person may cut a branch from a tree or a piece of cloth from a garment. Alternate translation: “to remove anyone who may want to help Tyre and Sidon” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## g199
This is the name of an island of the northern region of the Philistines. (See: [[translate-names]])

# 47:5
## u8ep
The abstract noun “baldness” can be stated as “bald.” “Gaza” represents the people. Alternate translation: “The people of Gaza will shave their heads bald” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## yv2n
The shaving of the entire head or portions of the head is a sign of great sorrow practiced by idol worshipers in nations such as Philistia. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## dw3l
Possible meanings are (1) the people will be silent because they are mourning or (2) this is a euphemism that means the enemy soldiers will kill them. (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

## m4h3
Yahweh uses a question to emphasize that the people of Ashkelon will never stop mourning. Yahweh speaks to the people as if he were speaking to one woman, who is a metaphor for the city itself. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “You will forever cut yourselves in mourning.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-apostrophe]])

## f7f4
The cutting of one’s skin was another practice done by the idol worshipers when they mourn the dead. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 47:6
## eck8
The Philistines speak to the sword of Yahweh as if it were a person who could hear them. Alternate translation: “Yahweh, you who are holding your sword! When will you stop striking us with your sword? Stop your sword and put it back in its scabbard!” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## m6j8
Yahweh punishing the Philistines by sending the army from the north is spoken of as if Yahweh were striking them with a sword. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## cid1
The people of the Philistines use this question to express their deep frustration about the devastation caused by their enemies. This may be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Please be silent!” or “Please, Yahweh, stop striking us with your sword!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## r4br
A sword makes a noise as it slashes back and forth. Asking the sword to be silent means to ask it to stop slashing and killing. Alternate translation: “you rest” or “you stop slashing” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## hg5n
This is the case used to protect the blade of a sword.

# 47:7
## x3p6
Here Jeremiah is speaking. He continues speaking of the sword of Yahweh as if it were a person who could rest. Yahweh punishing the Philistines by sending the army of the north is spoken of as if Yahweh were striking the people with a sword. This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “It cannot rest … along the sea.” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## w3uj
Jeremiah uses a question to mean that the sword cannot stop since Yahweh has commanded it to continue killing. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “It cannot stop, for Yahweh has commanded it. He has ordered … the sea.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## h6mu

## uj9q

# 48:6
## lhh3
The word “your” refers to the people of Moab.

## kv9q

# 48:7
## gkg1
The abstract noun “trust” can be stated as a verb. Alternate translation: “because you trusted” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## z18z
“in your own efforts” or “in your own work”

## v42e

## x4hy

# 48:8
## uxi3

## wca7

## e6c4

# 48:9
## jd6u
Helping the people is spoken of as if they would put wings on the people so that could fly away. Alternate translation: “Help the people of Moab to escape as if you were giving them wings to fly away” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## eq9z
The translation of the original language is uncertain. Some Bibles translate this as “Set up a tombstone for Moab, for the enemy will certainly destroy it.” Other Bibles translate it as “Put salt on the cities of Moab, for the enemy will completely destroy it.” Enemies through salt on ruins to keep thing from ever growing there again.

# 48:10
## c5zb
Here “blood” represents a person’s life. And “shedding blood” is an idiom that means to kill. Alternate translation: “from killing people” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 48:11
## lvs4
Here Yahweh speaks of Moab as if it were a man. Here it represents the people of Moab. All occurrences of “he” and “his” can be stated as “they,” “their,” or “them.” Alternate translation: “Even from their earliest times, the people of Moab have felt secure … They are … their wine … They have never gone … they tastes … their flavor” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## tpx9
For wine to taste good, it has to be left undisturbed for a long time and not poured from one pot to another. Yahweh compares the people of Moab, who are prosperous because no one has ever conquered them and taken them as captives, to wine that is undisturbed and has a good flavor. (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-simile]])

# 48:12
## mt13
“Listen carefully because there will be a time”

## p3kf
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## z48t
The enemy army defeating the people of Moab and taking them as captives is spoken of as if the people of Moab are wine and someone will come and pour them out and break the wine jars. Alternate translation: “I will send an army that will destroy them as if they were pouring out wine and breaking the jars” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## nky7
Here Yahweh speaks of Moab as if it were a man. Here it represents the people of Moab. All occurrences of “he,” “his,” and “him” in 48:11-12 can be stated as “they,” “their,” or “them.” Alternate translation: “send them … tip them over … their pots … their jars” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 48:13
## le7f
Here “Moab” represents the people. Alternate translation: “Then the people of Moab” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## biu2
Chemosh is the chief god of the Moabites. See how you translated this name in [Jeremiah 48:7](../48/07.md). (See: [[translate-names]])

## qrf9
The word “house” is a metonym for the family that lives in the house. In this case it refers to Israel’s descendants. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 2:4](../02/04.md). Alternate translation: “the Israelites” or “the Israelite people group” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## fr2l
Possible meanings are (1) this refers to the city of Bethel where the Israelites worshiped a golden calf or (2) Bethel is the name of a false god the Israelites worshiped.

# 48:14
## r8r7
Here Yahweh is speaking to the soldiers of Moab. Yahweh uses a question to rebuke the soldiers. Alternate translation: “No longer can you say, ‘We are soldiers, powerful fighting men.’” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 48:15
## z5yr
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “The enemy army will devastate Moab and attack its cities” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## b73e
The best men of Moab going to battle and being killed is spoken of as if they were animals taken to a place to be slaughtered. Alternate translation: “their fine young men will all be slaughtered” or “the enemy army will kill all of the finest men of Moab” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## x1zc
Here “king” refers to Yahweh. This can be stated in first person. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 46:18](../46/18.md). Alternate translation: “This is what I, the king, declare” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 48:16
## f7i9
These two sentences mean basically the same thing and emphasize that Moab’s destruction will happen very soon. Alternate translation: “Moab’s enemies will destroy her very soon” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## u5tf
Something terrible happening soon is spoken of as if calamity were something that could move quickly to a place. Alternate translation: “terrible things will happen very soon” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 48:17
## l1hb
Here the words “staff” and “rod” represent the power and political support that Moab provided to other nations. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Moab’s enemies have ended her great power” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## r5qs

Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## et9f

## f4y5

This simile means that when Yahweh punishes the people of Edom, it will be fierce and unexpected like when a lion attacks sheep. This can be stated in first person. Alternate translation: “When I punish the people of Edom, it will be fierce and unexpected like when a lion comes out of forest and attacks sheep in the pasture” (See: [[figs-simile]] and [[figs-123person]])

## k55q
This is land covered with grass where animals feed on the grass.

## l6pt

## j5u5
“who will challenge me” or “who will call me to account”

## y36a
Yahweh uses the question to show no one can defeat him. Here “shepherd” is a metaphor for “king” or “ruler.” This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “No king is able to resist me!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## aq6m

# 50:23
## y8x3

## vq6u

# 50:25
## v2ka

## qd2z
this is a building or room where weapons of war are stored

# 51:2
## bdq6
The word “her,” a metaphor that refers to Babylon as if it were a woman, is also a metonym for the people who live in Babylon. Alternate translation: “separate the people who live there from each other, send them in different directions, and make their land empty … foreigners will attack the people of Babylon” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## w4jj

# 51:4
## u6a8

# 51:5
## u9ey
The words “Israel” and “Judah” are metonyms for the people of Israel and Judah. This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “Yahweh of hosts, the God of the people of Israel and Judah, has not forsaken them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## t4nl
The land in which they live is spoken of as if it were a container and their offenses as if they were solid objects that could fill the container. The abstract noun “offenses” can be translated as a verb. Alternate translation: “they have offended the Holy One of Israel everywhere throughout the land” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 51:6
## ht2y
Babylon is spoken of as if it were a woman. The word “iniquity” is a metonym for God punishing the people for their iniquity. Alternate translation: “when Yahweh punishes Babylon” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## dia4
Babylon is spoken of as if it were a woman, and the iniquity of the people is spoken of as if Yahweh needed to pay back a debt. The word “her,” meaning Babylon, is a metonym for the people of Babylon. Alternate translation: “will punish the Babylonians as much as they deserve” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 51:7
## tj8i
The word “world” is a metonym for the people living in the nations surrounding Babylon. Possible meanings are (1) Making those people drunk could be a metaphor for Yahweh punishing those nations by allowing Babylon to conquer them. Alternate translation: “Babylon was a strong nation that Yahweh used to punish all the people of the surrounding nations severely” or (2) It could be a metaphor for those nations joining Babylon in worshiping idols and being violent. Alternate translation: “Babylon was a rich and powerful nation, and other nations wanted to become sinful just like it” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## bqq8
Babylon being a powerful nation that Yahweh used for his own purposes is spoken of as if Babylon were a golden cup in his hand. Here “hand” represents Yahweh’s power. (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## ye28
Possible meanings are (1) “Babylon defeated and destroyed other nations” or (2) “nations enjoyed her riches and power and became evil.” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## s9ql
unable to think clearly

## mjn3
The word “how” here shows that something that people thought was impossible has happened. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “We never thought that enemies could capture Babylon, but they have captured her … We never thought that Babylon could become a ruined place, but it has become a ruined place” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## epv5
The word “praise” is a metonym for the thing that people praised, and the word “earth” is a metonym for the people living on the earth. This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “enemies have seized Babylon, the nation that everyone on earth praised” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 51:42
## jlw8
Babylon’s enemies have overcome her. “Waves” represent many people groups overcoming Babylon. The words “sea” and “water” often represent the nations. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## xv1n
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “Its roaring waves have covered her” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 51:43
## i1ik
“Babylon’s cities”

# 51:44
## ua7k
Bel was the main god of the Babylonians and represents the whole country and the people that worshiped it. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## x7hy
Yahweh compares all the sacrifices and offerings to Bel to things he ate. (See: [[figs-personification]])

## b1d8
The many nations that come to Babylon to sacrifice to Bel is spoken of as a river that moves along. Alternate translation: “the people of other nations will no longer come in large groups” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 51:46
## e7mp
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “when you hear the news that is in the land” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## y5k9
The ruler is a metonym for the nations under his control. Alternate translation: “Nations will fight against other nations” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 51:47
## hlg9
“look” or “listen” or “pay attention to what i am about to tell you”

## hh7n
Future time is spoken of as if the “days are coming.” See how you translated this metaphor in [Jeremiah 7:32](../07/32.md). Alternate translation: “in the future … I will punish” or “there will be a time … when I will punish” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## x6k7
Removing the idols of Babylon is spoken of as if Yahweh would punish the idols. Alternate translation: “I will remove the carved idols of Babylon” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## m3al
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “the people of Babylon … their land … their slaughtered ones … their midst” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 51:48
## r764
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “over the Babylonians … come for them … the Babylonians have made … their land” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## a7da
Heaven and earth are viewed as if they were people. (See: [[figs-personification]])

## ry4n
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 51:49
## y6sf
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “the people of Babylon … their land … their slaughtered ones … their midst … over the Babylonians … come for them … the Babylonians have made … their land” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## mh52
“those whom the Babylonians’ enemies have killed in the land”

# 51:50
## rnu2
See [[JER-rnu2]]
## v78z
The sword is a metonym for war, in which soldiers use swords. Alternate translation: “You who have survived the war” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## esb3
The phrases “Call … to mind” and “come to mind” are idioms that mean to think about or remember. Alternate translation: “Though you are far away, think about Yahweh; think about Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 51:51
## l188
words that one person uses to offend another

## u8ak
The speakers’ reaction to people expressing disapproval of them is spoken of as if it were a cover placed over the speakers’ faces. Alternate translation: “our faces show that we are hurt because others disapprove of us” or “we cover our faces in shame because others disapprove of us” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 51:52
## gq6f
“look” or “listen” or “pay attention to what i am about to tell you”

## t1lg
Future time is spoken of as if the “days are coming.” See how you translated this metaphor in [Jeremiah 7:32](../07/32.md). Alternate translation: “in the future … I will punish” or “there will be a time … when I will punish” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ipn3
Yahweh speaks of himself by name to express the certainty of what he is declaring. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 1:8](../01/08.md). Alternate translation: “this is what Yahweh has declared” or “this is what I, Yahweh, have declared” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## h25y
Removing or destroying idols is spoken of as if Yahweh would punish the idols. Alternate translation: “I will destroy her carved idols” or “I will remove her carved idols” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## c2dr
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “their carved idols … their land … the Babylonians … their highest fortresses … to them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## z6xh
to cry out because of pain or sadness

# 51:53
## i44x
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “their highest fortresses … to them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## t773
This is a hypothetical situation that Yahweh does not expect to happen. Alternate translation: “Babylon will not go up … or fortify … destroyers will surely come from me” (See: [[figs-hypo]])

# 51:54
## j9es
The same thought is expressed in two different ways for emphasis. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 51:55
## i691
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “destroying the people of Babylon … their loud voices” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## rb5t
“The enemies of the people of Babylon … the noise of the enemies”

## hs3i
The waves of the ocean and of flooding rivers make a loud sound, and the enemies will make a very loud sound as they come. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 51:56
## bdd2
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “against the people of Babylon—yes, against Babylon!—and their warriors” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## sx3e
This phrase can be changed to have an active verb. Alternate translation: “her enemies have captured her warriors” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 51:57
## n6e9
The people of Babylon are spoken of as if they were the city itself, and the city is spoken of as if it were a woman. Alternate translation: “their princes, their sages, their officials, and their soldiers … their high gates … their aid … for them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## gc1t
They will be dead. See how you translated this in [Jeremiah 51:39](../51/39.md). (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

# 51:58
## qv76

# 51:59
## x35d
These are the names of men. (See: [[translate-names]])

## r4gy
“after he had been king for three years.” This “fourth” is the ordinal number for four. (See: [[translate-ordinal]])

# 51:60
## f3pz
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “that he had written about Babylon”

# 51:62
## ctw9
If your language has a word for animals that normally live with people, you might want to use it here, because [Jeremiah 51:37](../51/37.md) states that Babylon will be inhabited by wild animals.

# 51:64
## rl23
The stone and scroll will disappear in the water, the water will destroy the scroll, and Babylon will no longer look like a city or have buildings for people to live in. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## eg18
“will never again be a city”

## xm91
This means the Babylonians will fall because they are exhausted. Many translations do not include these words.

## q88p

## w5xq

# 52:9
## e3yw
Riblah was a town in the territory of Hamath. (See: [[translate-names]])

## y478
“decided how to punish him”

# 52:10
## rf5w
The eyes are a synecdoche for the whole person. The reader should also understand that others probably helped the king of Babylon kill Zedekiah’s sons. Alternate translation: “forced Zedekiah to watch as the king of Babylon slaughtered Zedekiah’s sons” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 52:11
## xk3k
“the king’s men made Zedekiah blind.” Use the common words for making a person blind. It is not clear that the king of Babylon took Zedekiah’s eyes out of his head. The reader should also understand that others probably helped the king of Babylon make Zedekiah blind.

## wdy8
the day of Zedekiah’s death

## e83x
Nebuchadnezzar had been king for eighteen years, four months, and nine days. This is the fifth month of the Hebrew calendar. It is during the dry season. The tenth day is near the beginning of August on Western calendars. (See: [[translate-hebrewmonths]] and [[translate-ordinal]])
